  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_955f42e2-b4a6-49b5-9902-d6965795b3c6'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer14066536416905160215">In 2007, the Center for Automation in Nanobiotech (CAN) outlined the hardware and software platform that would one day allow robots smaller than human cells to make medical diagnoses, conduct repairs, and even self-propagate.   <span id="freeText14066536416905160215" style="display:none">In 2007, the Center for Automation in Nanobiotech (CAN) outlined the hardware and software platform that would one day allow robots smaller than human cells to make medical diagnoses, conduct repairs, and even self-propagate. In the same year, the CBS network re-aired a program about the effects of propranolol on sufferers of extreme trauma. A simple pill, it had been discovered, could wipe out the memory of any traumatic event. At almost the same moment in humanity’s broad history, mankind had discovered the means for bringing about its utter downfall. And the ability to forget it ever happened. This is the sequel to the New York Times bestselling WOOL series.<br /><br />Contains First Shift, Second Shift, and Third Shift.</span>

<a href="/user/show/5935603-robert-rev-bob">Robert &#39;Rev. Bob&#39;</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">There's a mention early in the book - first chapter, I think - about life expectancy rising to about 150 in the pre-silo days. Given that the Crow's s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">There's a mention early in the book - first chapter, I think - about life expectancy rising to about 150 in the pre-silo days. Given that the Crow's story takes place before <i>Wool</i>, and considering how the threat to the silo is identified, my best guess is that she's the last remaining member of the original batch of "colonists." I also suspect that the story involving her is the one that culminated in the data-wipe mentioned in <i>Wool</i>.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The blurb I see (about CAN and nanomachines) is actually the beginning of the book, not a real summary.<br/><br/>Basically, once you've read Wool and know how t<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The blurb I see (about CAN and nanomachines) is actually the beginning of the book, not a real summary.<br /><br />Basically, once you've read Wool and know how that story ends, Shift goes back to the beginning to show you how the setting came to be, as well as a prior uprising and Solo's backstory. Dust rounds out the trilogy by finally picking up from the end of Wool (and Shift) to move all the story arcs forward.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_770195392" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/770195392" />
    <a title="Willow " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4529669-willow"><img alt="Willow " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472180488p2/4529669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/770195392?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willow " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willow " href="/user/show/4529669-willow">Willow </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781481983556" href="/book/show/17373706-shift">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer770195392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5613722077212709896"><b>*Buddy Read with Rachel*(BAVR)</b><br/><br/><br/>Have you ever been happily reading a good book? You’re about to find out what happens to the protagonist. Your heart is pounding with anticipation. When all of a sudden, out of nowhere, the book dumps you into a ridiculously long ass flashback. Everything comes to a complete and utter stop. It’s like being trapped in a meandering shaggy dog story. And of course you’ve got to read the damn flashback, because if you don’t you may not completely grasp the full ramifica</span>
  <span id="freeText5613722077212709896" style="display:none"><b>*Buddy Read with Rachel*(BAVR)</b><br /><br /><br />Have you ever been happily reading a good book? You’re about to find out what happens to the protagonist. Your heart is pounding with anticipation. When all of a sudden, out of nowhere, the book dumps you into a ridiculously long ass flashback. Everything comes to a complete and utter stop. It’s like being trapped in a meandering shaggy dog story. And of course you’ve got to read the damn flashback, because if you don’t you may not completely grasp the full ramifications of the ending. <br /><br />Well, <i>Shift</i> is a 600 page, doorstopper flashback. <br /><br />To be honest, I sort of hated the book even before I began. I wanted to go forwards, not backwards. <br /><br />But I tried to stay optimistic. After all, I loved <i>Wool</i>. I loved the nostalgia <i>Wool</i> generated in me for classic SciFi. I loved the suspense. The characters were cool. I was ready to take the plunge.<br /><br />Unfortunately, <i>Shift</i> is a long and tedious read. <br /><br />First off, there’s little suspense. After all, it's a prequel, so of course I knew basically what was going to happen. Howey also had the audacity to drag it out. He kept describing the same things over and over again. I started counting how many times he mentions a tomato, or tomato soup, or tomato paste. The dead guys looked like tomato sauce was on them. Howey also likes to talk about the circular staircase in the silo and overalls. Bored, I started to wonder what these overalls are made of? It better not be cotton? They don’t have the room. And speaking of room, how can they grow so much wheat? How are their computers lasting for centuries (mine usually crash after ten years). And what is the hierarchy for the powers that be? Even Donald doesn’t know that for sure and he lives there? <br /> <br />And the absolute worst was that lame ass, WTF reason they blew up the world? I couldn’t believe that nobody, and I mean nobody, questions Thurman and his cronies? You’ve got to be kidding me? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Don’t tell me that the US Air Force was willing to blow up their own US cities, because I don’t buy it. And what?? They couldn’t work on finding a way to stop the Nanos, or make a stand-off, like we did for years with Russia and nuclear bombs during the cold war? Their only solution was to blow up the world, otherwise they would just have years of back and forth Nano warfare? Are you serious? And what was all that crap at the end about how only one silo can survive because people would kill each other. What, people can’t work together? Only in Howey’s world. Can I say PLOT HOLE?! *end of rant* <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />In fact, there are several plot holes in <i>Shift</i>. Most of them have to do with character motivation and time. But let’s forget about that for a minute. Let’s talk about the characters.<br /><br /><b>Donald</b> -- God I hated that guy. I swear the whole book is him having a pity party. I could handle it in the beginning (after all, horrible things happen to him) but by the third book I wanted to rip his head off. I know Donald is supposed to be a sympathetic character because he was duped and deceived, but let’s face it, he purposely tried to stay uninformed. That was his goal, to bury his head in the sand, even when obvious hints were thrown his way. I think he’s one of the biggest, redundant and whiney, woobie characters I’ve read in a long time. And the thing that finally tips him over the edge made my eyes roll. Turman’s been slaughtering people for years and Don finally decides to do something about it because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he finally figured out that Anna purposely separated him from his wife? wtf?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Thurman</b> – Is a total nutcase. How come nobody notices that? I think that’s what drove me bonkers. Everybody was just so willing and happy to put Charles Manson in charge of national security. Supposedly Thurman is so charismatic, people just melt like butter and agree with him after just a few minutes. Like Rodney -- who went through the most bogus character arc I’ve seen in a while. Does Howey honestly expect us to believe that milquetoast Donald is the only person to try and stop Thurman? Give me a break? <br /> <br /> <b>Anna</b> – I kept wondering what the hell she saw in Donald? The trouble she goes to winning him baffled me.<br /><br /><b>Mission</b> – Was boring and had no personality. Of course, most of the characters didn’t have much of a personality. It probably didn’t help that in Mission’s scenes, Howey spends long passages describing life in the Silo one more time (like I didn’t get enough of that in <i>Wool</i>?)<br /> <br /><b>Solo</b> – His story is very sad. But let’s face it, we already knew what was going to happen to him. <br /><br /><b>Shadow</b> -- Was purposely put in the book to make Solo’s life even sadder. Grrr<br /><br /><b>Charlotte</b> -- Who’s that?<br /><br /><b>Helen</b> -- Made me think of June Cleaver. <br /> <br />In conclusion, this book annoyed me. I loved <i>Wool</i>. I wanted to read <i>Dust</i>, but now I’m not so sure. Howey should have left the past a mystery.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5613722077212709896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_560954108" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/560954108" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7170480-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363668715p2/7170480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/560954108?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/7170480-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer560954108" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15977695521872062874">Hugh Howey is a great idea man and the post-apocalyptic world of the Silos is a wonderful little playground with the potential for great stories. Unfortunately Howey's skills at character-building leave much to be desired and ultimately drags down the entire series.<br/> <br/>Here's some of the ways Howey fails at writing characters: he never tells us what anybody looks like. Every character sounds the same and has no identifying tics or habits. The relationship of just about every character to every othe</span>
  <span id="freeText15977695521872062874" style="display:none">Hugh Howey is a great idea man and the post-apocalyptic world of the Silos is a wonderful little playground with the potential for great stories. Unfortunately Howey's skills at character-building leave much to be desired and ultimately drags down the entire series.<br /> <br />Here's some of the ways Howey fails at writing characters: he never tells us what anybody looks like. Every character sounds the same and has no identifying tics or habits. The relationship of just about every character to every other character is told to us instead of shown. For example, we know Donald loves Helen because we're told he loves Helen, not because he acts any special way towards her beyond calling her Sweety or Honey. Mick is his best friend because were told he's his best friend (Mick slaps him on the back a lot so I guess that proves it). Anna wants Donald to herself because we're told...and so on.<br /> <br />As the main character Donald is as bland as they come, showing no particular intelligence or personality to distinguish himself from the other cast members, who themselves have no distinctive personalities. Because of this I had a very hard time either identifying or sympathizing with anybody beyond their incredibly horrific circumstances (the one exception being Solo but only because he REALLY got the shaft, so to speak).<br /> <br />Lately I seem to keep running into novels of good ideas and poor execution i.e. Justin Cronin's 'Passage' and Ernest Cline's 'Ready Player One'. It's disappointing and as a contrast i'd recommend Steven King's 'The Stand' as an example of the apocalypse done right with solid world-building and well drawn characters we come to love or hate. For me it's still the gold-standard. <br /><br />All this being said I'll still come back for the final chapter of WOOL because damn if it isn't an interesting place to explore. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15977695521872062874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_617626284" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/617626284" />
    <a title="Jonathan Terrington" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan-terrington"><img alt="Jonathan Terrington" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478230200p2/6313408.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/617626284?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Terrington" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Terrington" href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan-terrington">Jonathan Terrington</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781780891224" href="/book/show/17301301-shift">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi-challenge">fantasy-sci-fi-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=favourite-series">favourite-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=personal-favourites">personal-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan-terrington?shelf=action-challenge-2">action-challenge-2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer617626284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5168562509144320133"><br/><i>Shift</i>, as the impressive science fiction follow up to <i>Wool</i>, proves that Hugh Howey can write - and write well. This is a prequel, but is, in my opinion, better read after <i>Wool</i> in order to not destroy the unique aspects of reading Hugh Howey's first work. <br/><br/>I would say that together <i>Shift</i> and <i>Wool</i> appear as better versions of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6186357.The_Maze_Runner__Maze_Runner___1_' title='The Maze Runner (Maze Runner, #1) by James Dashner' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Maze Runner</a> series. Not only are they far more mature in their approach to their particular topics but they possess and infinite amount more plausibility and depth. The wa</span>
  <span id="freeText5168562509144320133" style="display:none"><br /><i>Shift</i>, as the impressive science fiction follow up to <i>Wool</i>, proves that Hugh Howey can write - and write well. This is a prequel, but is, in my opinion, better read after <i>Wool</i> in order to not destroy the unique aspects of reading Hugh Howey's first work. <br /><br />I would say that together <i>Shift</i> and <i>Wool</i> appear as better versions of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6186357.The_Maze_Runner__Maze_Runner___1_" title="The Maze Runner (Maze Runner, #1) by James Dashner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Maze Runner</a> series. Not only are they far more mature in their approach to their particular topics but they possess and infinite amount more plausibility and depth. The way in which <i>Shift</i> sets out the background to <i>Wool</i>, explaining how humanity could end up in silos is brilliant. It particularly works as an exploration of politics, weaponry, humanity's quest for self annihilation, versus the drive to survive. This stands alongside that sense of the contrast between the inner darkness versus the inner greatness of humanity. <br /><br />However, the one reason this receives a four star rating is that the characters lacked in comparison to the previous novel and were not as appealing to myself as a reader. For once I'm lost for words as to how to properly review all the aspects of this novel. Perhaps fatigue has destroyed me over this week. That said, I will recommend all serious readers give this book a go after they've tried <i>Wool</i>. I will be seriously looking forward to the next book myself. I do love quality science fiction and while I'm lacking something to fully capture and describe about the depth and lack of superficiality or immaturity in this novel I cannot find it. The best thing to do is simply recommend that others read these books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5168562509144320133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1996140534" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1996140534" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1996140534?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099580478" href="/book/show/17612908-shift">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=american-21st-c">american-21st-c</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1996140534" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5866086714659195217">UPDATED! PARANOID RANT NEAR THE END WITH SLIGHT SPOILER!<br/><br/>The second volume of Howey's Solo trilogy takes us back to the origin of the silos and introduces a few good guys - Donald in particular - a few bad guys - Thurman although we are not sure until Dust exactly how evil he is - and some ambiguous characters - Anna (well, you'll need to read Dust to see how this turns out). It is an interesting idea with the nanobots and the Noah's Arc concept. The suspense is done quite well with time shifting</span>
  <span id="freeText5866086714659195217" style="display:none">UPDATED! PARANOID RANT NEAR THE END WITH SLIGHT SPOILER!<br /><br />The second volume of Howey's Solo trilogy takes us back to the origin of the silos and introduces a few good guys - Donald in particular - a few bad guys - Thurman although we are not sure until Dust exactly how evil he is - and some ambiguous characters - Anna (well, you'll need to read Dust to see how this turns out). It is an interesting idea with the nanobots and the Noah's Arc concept. The suspense is done quite well with time shifting back and forth as the reality of the project slowly dawns on Donald. (Honestly, I wanted to slap him a few times for his naiveté.) For my reservations regarding the larger story line, you'll need to read my review of Dust. As for this story, it is carried primarily on the suspense that Howey builds throughout rather than character development which I felt was rather flat. Still, it is an interesting follow-up to Wool which I enjoyed peripherally and I was still intrigued enough to follow through with Dust.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />A quick followup to say that I did a little research and the idea of nanobots being used against the human body rather than for it may be a far more realistic scenario than I previously thought. Reading this article from 2015 about using AI to fight disease (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://hplusmagazine.com/2015/06/10/artificial-intelligence-as-a-supplement-to-the-human-immune-system/">http://hplusmagazine.com/2015/06/10/a...</a>) and this one from 2009 about immortality being achieved by 2040 (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.computerworld.com/article/2528330/app-development/nanotech-could-make-humans-immortal-by-2040--futurist-says.html">https://www.computerworld.com/article...</a>), I suppose that the ideas that Howey uses to underpin the manmade disaster that destroys the human race (other than those in the silos that we know of) is not as far off as I thought. This even more recent article shows, that in labs at least, we already have nanobots that can attack specific cells (in this case ones with cancer) (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2016/08/160815134945.htm">https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases...</a>) or there are these nanobots developed by Israel for brain diseases (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.algemeiner.com/2016/08/28/israeli-researchers-invent-mind-controlled-dna-nanobots-for-potential-treatment-of-brain-disorders-from-epilepsy-to-depression/">https://www.algemeiner.com/2016/08/28...</a>). It is not hard to imagine how this technology could easily be reprogrammed (like on the Black Mirror episode Hated in the Nation (S03E06)!) to kill good, healthy cells. OK, so I guess I am going to have a glass or three of wine before bed tonight... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="5866086714659195217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_748265479" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/748265479" />
    <a title="Alexis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3560581-alexis"><img alt="Alexis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445421348p2/3560581.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/748265479?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexis" href="/user/show/3560581-alexis">Alexis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer748265479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15759220178846586239">I really wanted to like this book, I really did. But unfortunately, the writer falls into the same traps he did in Wool. Like Wool, this book tells a fantastic story for the first 2/3 - in this case, I really did love the way the conspiracy built piece by piece before your eyes, and I enjoyed slowly putting it together with a lot of "OMG" moments. If the book had ended after just that, it would have been a 5 star review. However, in this novel Howey seems not to be able to restrain himself from </span>
  <span id="freeText15759220178846586239" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this book, I really did. But unfortunately, the writer falls into the same traps he did in Wool. Like Wool, this book tells a fantastic story for the first 2/3 - in this case, I really did love the way the conspiracy built piece by piece before your eyes, and I enjoyed slowly putting it together with a lot of "OMG" moments. If the book had ended after just that, it would have been a 5 star review. However, in this novel Howey seems not to be able to restrain himself from going overboard with big reveals and too-clever tricks. It seems like he's always waiting around the corner with a BUT WAIT THERE'S MORE moment, and after a while I just developed 'reveal fatigue' I guess.<br /><br />It doesn't help that the more Howey reveals, the less the conspiracy makes sense. Suspending disbelief is easy when the writer leaves much of the details to your imagination, but Howey can't do that - he has to explain every little thing and eventually it falls apart. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[For instance, why was destroying the world the best and only solution? I mean it seems like these brilliant scientists he gathered could come up with <i>something</i> else to try? And why were three people, only one of whom was in government, able to convince an entire Air Force division to drop nuclear bombs on Atlanta?! It just seems a little bizarre that the secret cabal we've been hearing about since the beginning of Wool ended up being a Dixiecrat junior senator and a couple of doctors.<br /><br />I also thought that the romance bit was unnecessary and kind of ridiculous. It killed the mood for me when Donald was pining away for Helen, and I thought it was unrealistic and kind of pathetic that he got stuck on that even when Helen was dead for 200 years. It made Donald into a very weak character, and suddenly it was hard to be sympathetic with him as that story unfolded. As he started falling apart I found myself rolling my eyes more than I was feeling sorry for him. And finally in that scene with Anna when he kills her, it was <i>literally</i> the stupidest thing he could possibly do, and yet, he did it, so I just was totally unable to have sympathy for him past that point... after I lost emotional interest in Donald, the rest kind of fell apart for me. I just didn't care about the characters anymore.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I also thought the entire thing was bogged down by the way-too-long chapters on Solo. Like, I really didn't need to know the details of Solo's bowel movements through the years he was alone. Those chapters seemed to serve no purpose but to fill space and they just bogged down the story, after a while I was just skipping through to get back to Silo 1.<br /><br />Finally, the biggest disappointment here was that Howey was as devoted in this book as he was in Wool to forcing a happy ending. He's created a world of despair and hopelessness, and then it seems like he can't bring himself to follow through with it - the endings of the books all seem forced and fake. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Bringing Juliette back into this at the end served no real purpose and wasn't related to the internal narrative in this book. It was just a set-up for the happy ending in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17855756.Dust__Silo___3_" title="Dust (Silo, #3) by Hugh Howey" rel="nofollow noopener">Dust</a>. Forcing your characters into a jarring scene where they skip off into the sunset ruins the entire thing when it is not the ending you've tracked them into throughout the entire story. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It is a sign of an undisciplined writer, and unfortunately that lack of discipline overshadowed my enjoyment of this story.<br /><br />Overall, this book was very disappointing - what could have been an amazing conspiracy story about an irrevocable act by a group of madmen fell beneath the hand of an unskilled story-teller. The plot became more and more incoherent, and the 'big reveals' got more and more showy and silly. I wanted to hear about this cabal that murdered 7 billion people <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[if they even did that, which the writer never seems to be able to decide on <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, not hear Hugh Howey pat himself on the back for being super clever. I won't be reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17855756.Dust__Silo___3_" title="Dust (Silo, #3) by Hugh Howey" rel="nofollow noopener">Dust</a>, because I know where it's going and I know it would make the entire trilogy incoherent and silly. It's frustrating, because this could have been a really groundbreaking sci-fi story, but instead it was an amateur overtelling of details that didn't make any sense.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15759220178846586239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13716524-evelina-avalinahsbooks?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13716524-evelina-avalinahsbooks?shelf=society">society</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1970641268" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15356858432462131969">It is truly a sorry affair that <i>
  <b>most sequels just do not turn out right.</b>
</i><br/>Well, alright, <i>
  <b>not most. Some.</b>
</i><br/>Can we settle on <i>
  <b>a lot</b>
</i>? <br/><br/>I can start naming now,<br/>(The sequel to Blood Red Road, Metro 2034...)<br/>and I am sure you can carry on with this list to eternity. In fact, please carry on in the comments! I would really love to find out <i>
  <b>what sequel drove you mad</b>
</i>.<br/><br/>So basically, I think we can draw the conclusion that <i>
  <b>you pretty much have to be J.K. Rowling not to mess up the sequels.</b>
</i><br/><br/>So am </span>
  <span id="freeText15356858432462131969" style="display:none">It is truly a sorry affair that <i>
  <b>most sequels just do not turn out right.</b>
</i><br>Well, alright, <i>
  <b>not most. Some.</b>
</i><br>Can we settle on <i>
  <b>a lot</b>
</i>? <br><br>I can start naming now,<br>(The sequel to Blood Red Road, Metro 2034...)<br>and I am sure you can carry on with this list to eternity. In fact, please carry on in the comments! I would really love to find out <i>
  <b>what sequel drove you mad</b>
</i>.<br><br>So basically, I think we can draw the conclusion that <i>
  <b>you pretty much have to be J.K. Rowling not to mess up the sequels.</b>
</i><br><br>So am I surprised that <i>
  <b>Shift</b>
</i> wasn't really a charming sequel to <i>
  <b>
    <a href="http://avalinahsbooks.space/wool-hugh-howey/?utm_source=Goodreads&amp;utm_medium=Review&amp;utm_campaign=Shift" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Wool</a>
  </b>
</i>?<br>No, dear friends, <i>
  <b>I am not. But am I disappointed?</b>
</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1474396112ra/20593934.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I don't really know where to begin with this book, or frankly - <i>
  <b>IF </b>
</i>I should begin at all, cause you can see I just gave it 1 star, <i>
  <b>which I pretty much never do</b>
</i>, so you must be getting the vibe that not only did this book not satisfy me, it probably even enraged me.<br><br>In fact, <b>
  <i>if that book had been a game, I would have rage quit.</i>
</b><br><br>This book has many very unfortunate factors that made me hate it. Part of it was that it was <i>
  <b>a sequel to a mind-blowing book</b>
</i>. But it's not just that! The plot dragged and dragged and dragged... I can't think of a book that has tortured me quite so lately.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1485586342ra/21829555.gif" alt="" width="375" height="159" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If I had to compare it with <i>
  <b><a href="http://avalinahsbooks.space/wool-hugh-howey/?utm_source=Goodreads&amp;utm_medium=Review&amp;utm_campaign=Shift" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Wool</a>,</b>
</i> well, first of all, the main character is such a wimp that you can't even. Literally. Second – okay, the first book <i>
  <b>is</b>
</i> quite depressing. But it's also uplifting in a way, cause they keep fighting. Nobody gives up. Shift though? What happens to the guy is <i>
  <b>so depressing</b>
</i>, that <i>
  <b>if you have any empathy at all,</b>
</i> like even the amount sea critters have, <i>
  <b>you will probably get sorely depressed from just touching the book</b>
</i>, much less reading it.<br>(Hint: I totally did.)<br><br>Unfortunately, I can't find much more to say about this book. It's a non-story completely. I know it will not remain in my mind at all, because, well, <i>
  <b>pretty much nothing happened.</b>
</i> Half of it was a retelling of what happened in the first book, half told stuff about a guy I honestly don't care to hear about, and intermingled in between all of that, there was <i>
  <b>maybe</b>
</i> a little bit about how or why the silos were actually built. Alright, I'll give you that – there <i>
  <b>is </b>
</i>the backstory. But hey. I could have saved so much more time (and brain cells) if that was just given to me in like 10 pages.<br><br>Now you will say, <i>
  <b>Evelina, dear friend, WHY DID YOU NOT DNF??</b>
</i><br>Well, now. I <i>
  <b>actually</b>
</i> have a pretty good reason for that.<br><br>You might know <i>
  <b>I do not DNF almost ever</b>
</i>, but this time I was buddy-reading this with my mom. And since I was taking my sweet time, she kept whining about wanting to talk about the story and just tortured me into finishing it! It was terrible :D abuse, I tell you! <i>
  <b>Here's a GIF of me </b>
  <b>(superstar!)</b>
</i> to show you how I felt while reading this book – I think it's a better outlet than text:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1495871574ra/22862364.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now you’ve seen it all.<br><br><a href="http://avalinahsbooks.space/shift-hugh-howey/?utm_source=Goodreads&amp;utm_medium=Review&amp;utm_campaign=Shift" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Read Post On My Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/avalinahsbooks/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">My Bookstagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/AvalinahsBooks" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookish Twitter</a></span>

<div id="review_1441304510" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1441304510" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1441304510?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/17391185-shift">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<div id="review_787435548" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/787435548" />
    <a title="John Carter McKnight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2410586-john-carter-mcknight"><img alt="John Carter McKnight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380249466p2/2410586.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/787435548?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Carter McKnight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Carter McKnight" href="/user/show/2410586-john-carter-mcknight">John Carter McKnight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer787435548" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer570090743422589856">Last night I was tempted to give this book two stars out of sheer frustration, but that wouldn't be fair. Yes, it's nowhere near as good as its predecessor, the excellent _Wool_. And yes, it's not what you'd call really good. But I did read it straight through, and with no regrets.<br/><br/>What Shift does well is provide context and explanations for much of the culture and history (or lack thereof) that we saw in Wool, and that's cool. As a sort of Simarillion, this prequel book works. The last few pages</span>
  <span id="freeText570090743422589856" style="display:none">Last night I was tempted to give this book two stars out of sheer frustration, but that wouldn't be fair. Yes, it's nowhere near as good as its predecessor, the excellent _Wool_. And yes, it's not what you'd call really good. But I did read it straight through, and with no regrets.<br /><br />What Shift does well is provide context and explanations for much of the culture and history (or lack thereof) that we saw in Wool, and that's cool. As a sort of Simarillion, this prequel book works. The last few pages also nicely set up the final volume of the trilogy. And, as with Wool, it's fun to track the many different ways in which the title fits the stories. <br /><br />What Shift doesn't do well is make sense. Or provide interesting, likeable characters. Or have a very good plot-to-page-count ratio. <br /><br />Shift is three stories: one short one of an early silo uprising told from the POV of a silo porter. That one's quite good, and the highlight of the book. One is the backstory of Solo from Wool: there's nothing new here; we got the picture just fine from Wool. And the story's ending is so crassly manipulative, so obvious, as to be downright disgusting. The whole arc didn't need to be there. <br /><br />The third is the story of one of the sort-of-unwitting creators of the silos, from the project's founding through his repeated thaws from suspended animation to address crises in the silos, up to the events ending Wool. This should have been terrific material, but failed on many counts. Donald is almost entirely passive in the face of world-ending events, just kind of bobbing along. He makes terrible decisions, not out of tragic flaws, but either from cognitive impairment or just not wanting to think too hard or take action. <br /><br />It's around Donald that Howey's writing fails: we're told repeatedly of Donald's epic passion for his wife, but all we see is a cool and distant relationship with someone vaguely unsympathetic. He uses this twoo wuv to rebuff the constant advances of the hot sociopath - but we never see why she might be interested in this nebbish. <br /><br />Generally, unlike in Wool, so much makes no sense here that it destroys immersion in the book. It's just not possible to imagine that the project could have been built as described (we're to believe that construction workers didn't talk to each other, and nobody bothered to look at 5000% cost overruns on everything), nor that the triggering event could have happened without *anyone* outside the conspiracy including an entire military chain of command getting asked if the mission was authorized. <br /><br />And, if everything's been thought through and sanitized, down to the encyclopedias for the inner circle, how come all the children are raised with books from before the apocalypse? <br /><br />Finally, even positing nano/bio mind control, Howey's society has no storytellers, no artists, not a single person who decorates their body or their uniform. These are not humans. Tell me they're Venusian Slime Molds, and I'll go along with you. But those are some of humanity's deepest traits. Tell me that you've eradicated those but kept farming and industrial labor, and sorry, I'm walking away. <br /><br />I'll read Dust, the final volume, but I'm not in any great hurry now. </span>
  <a data-text-id="570090743422589856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer490816216813865443">Continuing the saga of the Silo. . . .<br/><br/>I loved Wool, I loved this book! So many questions after reading Wool. Why, for instance, were there no elevators? This book addresses that - sort of. (I wonder if the author received enough emails about that topic that he felt he ought to mention it in his next book?) Shift answers a few Woolly questions, but asks quite a few more questions. I think that every few pages I was going "OMG!"<br/><br/>This book is more of a prequel to Wool but should definitely be read a</span>
  <span id="freeText490816216813865443" style="display:none">Continuing the saga of the Silo. . . .<br /><br />I loved Wool, I loved this book! So many questions after reading Wool. Why, for instance, were there no elevators? This book addresses that - sort of. (I wonder if the author received enough emails about that topic that he felt he ought to mention it in his next book?) Shift answers a few Woolly questions, but asks quite a few more questions. I think that every few pages I was going "OMG!"<br /><br />This book is more of a prequel to Wool but should definitely be read after Wool to prevent spoilers from ruining the story. This is the story of the Silo's beginnings. It starts many years before Wool, but then ends at the same time as Wool ends. Shift covers the lives of several people and a few of them are familiar. It plunged me right back into that world and I started once again dreaming of living in a silo every night. It's not often a book gets into my dreams, but this one certainly did. That must be part of the universal appeal of this book. Something in this book is definitely speaking to part of my brain.<br /><br />Looking forward to reading Dust. I'm not sure where this is heading.<br /><br />(BTW - it also makes for some fun conversation. Last night, for instance, my husband mentioned to me if I thought there would be fewer uprisings in the Silo if they had more reading material, maybe some fun magazines like Silo Living? That led to Living in the Mids, Up Top Life, Better Silos and (uh?) Silos, Down Deep Life and then eventually we realized there had to have been at least one uprising on the shortfall of TP (paper, after all, is such a precious commodity).<br /><br />Recommended for all Wool lovers out there.</span>

<div id="review_524592349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/524592349" />
    <a title="Timothy Ward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4867952-timothy-ward"><img alt="Timothy Ward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319336249p2/4867952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/524592349?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy Ward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy Ward" href="/user/show/4867952-timothy-ward">Timothy Ward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=indie">indie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4867952-timothy-ward?shelf=outread-aubrey">outread-aubrey</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer524592349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12088291297164160274">REVIEW SUMMARY: The sequel trilogy to the best seller, Wool Omnibus, which takes a leap back in time to show how the chaos started.<br/><br/>BRIEF SYNOPSIS: A silo architect finds out too late what he’s been building, loses track of his wife and memories, and must uncover the secret behind the silo in order to make everything right.<br/><br/>MY RATING: 3.5 stars<br/><br/>MY REVIEW<br/>PROS: Strong beginning; empathy for major characters; challenging philosophical themes about war and sacrifice to survive as a human race.<br/><br/>CONS: Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText12088291297164160274" style="display:none">REVIEW SUMMARY: The sequel trilogy to the best seller, Wool Omnibus, which takes a leap back in time to show how the chaos started.<br /><br />BRIEF SYNOPSIS: A silo architect finds out too late what he’s been building, loses track of his wife and memories, and must uncover the secret behind the silo in order to make everything right.<br /><br />MY RATING: 3.5 stars<br /><br />MY REVIEW<br />PROS: Strong beginning; empathy for major characters; challenging philosophical themes about war and sacrifice to survive as a human race.<br /><br />CONS: Third Shift (Book Three) slowed the story way down with minor revelations and sparse action.<br /><br />BOTTOM LINE: While the first half gave hope that this sequel could surpass Wool Omnibus, the story went downhill from there. Shift is still recommended for Wool fans, and it will not kill interest in reading the concluding volume even though it did not meet expectations.<br /><br />(Spoiler Warning: This review will have spoilers for people who have not read Wool, and only general spoilers for those who have yet to read Shift. Reviews for First Shift: Legacy and Second Shift: Order can be seen at the reviewer’s home page.)<br /><br />Shift Omnibus is made up of three books, First Shift – Legacy, Second Shift – Order, and Third Shift – Pact.<br /><br />First Shift – Legacy starts out with a young congressman, Donald, who has aspirations for greatness but finds out that his plans are not what those up the chain-of-command have in store for him. Donald is given the task of building an underground silo — just in case recent nanotechnology development continues to escalate into a world war — and is assigned to work with an old flame from his college days. This old flame flirts just like she always did, and he begins to wonder if she is manipulating events to keep him away from his wife. The race for Donald to figure out what he is really building and how to make sure he doesn’t lose his wife in the process is very exciting and emotional, reminiscent of the turmoil in Wool 1 and even made a few reviewers wonder what this series would have been like had it started with this book. The conclusion of First Shift – Legacy wows as much as it breaks one’s heart.<br /><br />Second Shift – Order continues to pound a hammer on the heart strings as it further unfolds the mystery of the silos and the over-arching moral dilemma of killing in order to save lives. The revelations mix perfectly with the questions posed about what you would do in Senator Thurman’s situation. The new POV character, a porter named Mission Jones, has a strong character arc of his own that is only slightly less impacting as Donald’s. Here, the author writes a tight plot exploring Mission’s inner struggle of hating to be a burden on anyone.<br /><br />This book’s inclusion of Mission Jones is an outstanding compliment to the overall plot and thematic question of whether humanity should adapt a survival-of-the-fittest mentality. Mission has a revelation: “Everyone was trying to get to where they didn’t need one another. And how exactly was that supposed to help them all get along?” This is the opposite side to Thurman’s stance that the people inside the silos don’t need the people who were killed above ground in order for their human-race “saving” experiment to be successful.<br /><br />The conclusion to Mission’s arc is also emotional and surprising. Senator Thurman makes a powerful argument for the establishment and goal of the silos, and it seems like Donald is not going to be able to overcome a system that treats people like numbers instead of integral pieces of a whole.<br /><br />Third Shift – Pact has an emotional beginning, but stalls as it shows snippets of a young man, Jimmy, growing up alone in a silo post-uprising. While this reader appreciates seeing the origin story of one of the remaining characters in Wool, the frank analysis of his story in this book is that he had twice as much page time as he should have. The discovery of self and finding of a partner in a stray cat were emotionally engaging, but the time spent holed up and then wandering made the end of this book a burden to finish.<br /><br />Jimmy’s revelation illustrated the point of his story within this omnibus, “Man wasn’t meant to live alone.” This reader just wishes the illustration of that point was more exciting, and maybe a letdown in Donald’s arc contributed to the lack of balance between introspection and intrigue.<br /><br />Donald’s arc in Third Shift – Pact did not surprise enough to match the excitement of the first two books. It was already clear that he was angry with Thurman and Anna, and so his solution to those problems was only mildly surprising, and a little more disappointing. Donald has gone through a very difficult life, but his decisions in Third Shift went a direction that lessened empathy and support. On top of that, the silo numbers became confusing as far as their relation to the plot’s mystery.<br /><br />To wrap up, Shift Omnibus took bold strides in expanding the conspiracy inherent in the world of the author’s Silo Saga, but a weak ending stole the momentum and interest established in the first two books. While Wool set this series up to be a lifetime favorite, Shift — and Third Shift specifically — tempered expectations significantly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12088291297164160274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_883815425" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/883815425" />
    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/883815425?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=apocalypse">apocalypse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer883815425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17729257274830233221"><b>Warning: Spoilers for "Wool", the previous book in the series.</b><br/><br/>While the title "Shift" actually refers to the time that one works, it's definitely a shift in the storytelling of the series. This volume goes back in time and tells the story of how and somewhat why the universe of "Wool" exists. It is a tale of politics, paranoia and nanotechnology. Yes the world ends in a somewhat SF cliche way, but it is still told with a great amount of talent, which makes those cliches forgivable. <br/><br/>Although we g</span>
  <span id="freeText17729257274830233221" style="display:none"><b>Warning: Spoilers for "Wool", the previous book in the series.</b><br /><br />While the title "Shift" actually refers to the time that one works, it's definitely a shift in the storytelling of the series. This volume goes back in time and tells the story of how and somewhat why the universe of "Wool" exists. It is a tale of politics, paranoia and nanotechnology. Yes the world ends in a somewhat SF cliche way, but it is still told with a great amount of talent, which makes those cliches forgivable. <br /><br />Although we get nearly all our questions answered and some fascinating tales are told, "Shift" lacks a couple of properties that made "Wool" such a great read. Firstly, while characterisation is still great, I found that I had little to no empathy for the main character, Donald. How can you connect with someone who is a workaholic, ignores his wife and who was lucky enough to know some influential people to get into a position to be exploited? Even his later actions in the novel, while they do fit his character as being naive and ham-fisted, do not endear me to this man at all. And I don't dislike him. I just feel meh about him. Luckily later on we get the back story of one of the great characters from "Wool", Solo or Jimmy. <br /><br />What is also lacking in this volume is some of the great and beautiful scenes I talked about in my review of "Wool". This may be due to my non-engagement with the main character and my wish to get through those sections efficiently and quickly, but this volume did seem to hold a greater precedence with plot and pace rather than having those small moments interspersed between.<br /><br />But despite these criticisms "Shift" is still a great SF read and complements "Wool" by answering a lot of the questions that came about in that novel. I look forward to reading the next volume "Dust" and being reunited with the great characters from Silo 18, especially Juliette. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17729257274830233221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_712842252" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/712842252" />
    <a title="Ferdy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy"><img alt="Ferdy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487898103p2/4596358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/712842252?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferdy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferdy" href="/user/show/4596358-ferdy">Ferdy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781780891224" href="/book/show/17301301-shift">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=interesting">interesting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=dumb-science">dumb-science</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=good">good</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=frustrating">frustrating</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4596358-ferdy?shelf=set-in-future">set-in-future</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer712842252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17599519894289448363"><b>Spoilers</b><br/><br/>Really good. I enjoyed the first two stories in <i>Shift</i> but the best story by far was the last one (<i>Third Shift</i>), as it focused on one of the more interesting arcs from <i>Wool</i>.<br/><br/>-I was initially disappointed that <i>Shift</i> was a prequel, I wanted to read more about the characters that were in <i>Wool</i>, especially Juliette, Solo and the kids. Thankfully, it didn't take too long to get invested in the prequel story or the new characters — although the characters weren't quite as endearing as those in <i>Wo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17599519894289448363" style="display:none"><b>Spoilers</b><br /><br />Really good. I enjoyed the first two stories in <i>Shift</i> but the best story by far was the last one (<i>Third Shift</i>), as it focused on one of the more interesting arcs from <i>Wool</i>.<br /><br />-I was initially disappointed that <i>Shift</i> was a prequel, I wanted to read more about the characters that were in <i>Wool</i>, especially Juliette, Solo and the kids. Thankfully, it didn't take too long to get invested in the prequel story or the new characters — although the characters weren't quite as endearing as those in <i>Wool</i>.<br /><br />-It was great finally getting some answers and a more complete picture of 1. The conception of the Silos 2. The world outside 3. The other Silos and 4. The end goal of the Silos. <br />I didn't quite believe the reasoning behind a lot of things that were revealed but I guess they weren't totally implausible.<br /><br />-The characters were a bit of a hit and miss for me. The main character, Donald, was hard to like at first — he was just so weepy, emotional, and annoying. He was meant to be a congressman but he acted way too naive and weak. He should have had a bit of strength, instead most of the time he acted like a scared little boy. Everyone around him manipulated him really easily, it was kind of ridiculous. Even though he was a frustrating character, he did grow on me by the end, and I eventually kind of understood why he had such an emotional reaction to everything.<br /><br />-I knew Troy would turn out to be Donald, it was really obvious. <br /><br />-It was somewhat sad that Donald was separated from his wife, Helen, because of Anna's manipulation. His reaction to finding out Helen married his best friend and had kids/grandkids was a little odd though — he kept saying how much he loved Helen but he never actually showed it. He seemed to have more passion and reaction to Anna, so I didn't really understand his utter devastation about losing Helen. I would have expected him to be a bit depressed but the way he broke down didn't make much sense, his love for Helen never really rang true especially when he was lusting after Anna so much. <br /><br />-Anna was a desperate manipulative cow. Mick was a selfish back stabbing douche. Hated them both.<br /><br />-It was dumb how Thurman and the other leaders just killed off loads of people whenever there was a slight problem. It just made the whole 50 Silos seem pointless, they may as well have just built a couple and let them sort out their own problems.<br /><br />-Mission's story was quite boring. And the Great Uprising was underwhelming.<br /><br />-Loved Jimmy's POV even though it was really depressing.<br /><br />-Some of the science with the freezing and nanotechnology seemed quite far fetched. <br /><br />-It only took 3 years to build all 50 Silos? Yea, that was hard to believe.<br /><br />-What happened to Silo 40 and the surrounding Silos? Why was Donald cool with destroying Silo 40 when he had such a hard time shutting down Silo 12? Why didn't he feel guilty about killing everyone in Silo 40 especially when he could have easily avoided it without his colleagues being any the wiser? <br />When it came to Silo 12 he didn't really have a choice about shutting it down, since it was his job… But with Silo 40 no-one else knew for certain that it had survivors so he didn't really have to do anything about them since it wasn't expected of him. It was just weird how he had no problem killing the people in 40 when he basically had a break down for the doing the same thing to 12.<br /><br />-What happened to the remaining people inside Silo 12? Were they all killed? How exactly was Silo 12 shut down? Was it the white fog? Or was the building itself destroyed? Or was it both?<br /><br />-What exactly happened in Silo 17 that caused the open airlock and mass hysteria? Was it because of the Cleaning? Did someone find out about the other Silos? <br /><br />-Why didn't Jimmy die when Donald shut his Silo down with that poison white fog?<br /><br />-What happened to the rest of the world? Was it just Georgia that was destroyed? Or was it all of America? What happened in other countries?<br /><br />-The excuse for not unfreezing/waking up the women was weak. As if men wouldn't get into fights without the presence of women.<br /><br />-The writing in <i>Shift</i> was much better than that of <i>Wool</i>.<br /><br />All in all, a great story though some of the characters were lacking.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17599519894289448363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15931187063704275254">Very savvy of the author to pen Shift after releasing Wool. The latter scooped me in immediately, whereas Shift took somewhat longer to lure me into its clutches. That's okay, I was already invested and there is a lot of information to be gleaned here. To those of you who want your Wool-ish questions answered, you can find them in Shift.<br/><br/>You will learn how the silos came into being and why. Shrinks are in charge of the insanity (I love that!). Working stiffs are working a never-ending mind-numbin</span>
  <span id="freeText15931187063704275254" style="display:none">Very savvy of the author to pen Shift after releasing Wool. The latter scooped me in immediately, whereas Shift took somewhat longer to lure me into its clutches. That's okay, I was already invested and there is a lot of information to be gleaned here. To those of you who want your Wool-ish questions answered, you can find them in Shift.<br /><br />You will learn how the silos came into being and why. Shrinks are in charge of the insanity (I love that!). Working stiffs are working a never-ending mind-numbing misery of shifts, following orders, obediently taking drugs to make them forget, but forget what? Silo 1 - responsible for humanity's very survival, population control management by lottery, the deep freeze, going dark, time becoming strange. And as always, the up top, the mids, and the down deep, only this time with shadows. 'It was meant to be this way.' <br /><br />Can't wait to see what Hugh Howey has in store for us with Dust. Loving the story!<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2366718831" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8158425559933753073">I very rarely read science fiction but this series by Hugh Howey sounded so interesting I gave it a chance. The first book was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13453029.Wool_Omnibus__Silo___1_' title='Wool Omnibus (Silo, #1) by Hugh Howey' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wool Omnibus</a> which was a 5 star read for me and this second book in the series didn't disappoint either.<br/> It's one of those stay up all night I just can't put it down kind of books. The characters are so well crafted and believable even under the bizarre circumstances they are trying their best to survive. <br/><br/>The premise is a future time when a group of survivors are living </span>
  <span id="freeText8158425559933753073" style="display:none">I very rarely read science fiction but this series by Hugh Howey sounded so interesting I gave it a chance. The first book was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13453029.Wool_Omnibus__Silo___1_" title="Wool Omnibus (Silo, #1) by Hugh Howey" rel="nofollow noopener">Wool Omnibus</a> which was a 5 star read for me and this second book in the series didn't disappoint either.<br /> It's one of those stay up all night I just can't put it down kind of books. The characters are so well crafted and believable even under the bizarre circumstances they are trying their best to survive. <br /><br />The premise is a future time when a group of survivors are living in a silo deep in the ground after some sort of dooms day event has occurred. Perilous events constantly threaten their survival which makes for fascinating reading. Can't wait to buy the next books in the series to see what happens.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8158425559933753073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_563016468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/563016468" />
    <a title="D.J. Gelner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15241318-d-j-gelner"><img alt="D.J. Gelner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354830915p2/15241318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/563016468?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D.J. Gelner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D.J. Gelner" href="/user/show/15241318-d-j-gelner">D.J. Gelner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer563016468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15868730838049846077">Immediately after finishing the <i>Wool</i> series, I was pleasantly surprised to find that Hugh Howey had finished <i>Third Shift</i>, which meant that <i>Shift Omnibus</i> was complete, and I could dive right into it.<br/><br/><i>Shift</i> provides some much-needed backstory for the <i>Wool</i> books, namely who designed the Silos, why, and most importantly, WHAT THE HELL IS GOING ON? (Sort of...more on that later...).<br/><br/>Hugh manages to do it all with his usual engrossing style. Dare I say I'm pleased to be able to see his evolution as a wr</span>
  <span id="freeText15868730838049846077" style="display:none">Immediately after finishing the <i>Wool</i> series, I was pleasantly surprised to find that Hugh Howey had finished <i>Third Shift</i>, which meant that <i>Shift Omnibus</i> was complete, and I could dive right into it.<br /><br /><i>Shift</i> provides some much-needed backstory for the <i>Wool</i> books, namely who designed the Silos, why, and most importantly, WHAT THE HELL IS GOING ON? (Sort of...more on that later...).<br /><br />Hugh manages to do it all with his usual engrossing style. Dare I say I'm pleased to be able to see his evolution as a writer through the entire series; as well-written as all of the <i>Wool</i> books were from the start, I think in <i>Shift</i> he takes his craft to a whole different level.<br /><br />I might disagree with a couple of characters' motivations toward the end of the book, but they make sense logically--if anything, I'm transposing what I would do in those situations without putting myself in that person's shoes. In other words, I was reading lazily.<br /><br />I have to admit, though, I woke up this morning having completed 80% of it, thinking that I would read a little and be off to writing.<br /><br />I finished the remaining 20% in a single sitting.<br /><br />It leaves an opening for <i>Dust</i>, which I assume will be the thrilling finale. Just what exactly lies beyond the Silos (pun intended)? And though the ostensible motive for constructing the Silos has been revealed, something tells me our crafty author isn't showing us all the cards just yet...<br /><br />So be warned: if you have a job where face time is valued and reading is frowned upon, wait until the weekend to pickup the <i>Wool</i> and <i>Shift</i> books. <br /><br />But if you like well-paced writing, gobs of suspense, fleshed-out characters, and a fearless author, I'd highly recommend starting with <i>Wool I</i> and seeing where it takes you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15868730838049846077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Russ Melrose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Russ Melrose" href="/user/show/21532539-russ-melrose">Russ Melrose</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5649579370152761899">I will admit, I struggled to get through Shift. While the writing is quite good, I never felt it was as compelling or interesting as Wool. Shift felt more like an addendum to Wool than a novel that could stand by itself. I enjoyed getting some answers about what happened in Wool, yet it wasn't enough to keep me interested.<br/><br/>My biggest problem was that I didn't care about the characters, especially the main character, Donald. Before the silos came into play, Donald was a senator. As far as what was</span>
  <span id="freeText5649579370152761899" style="display:none">I will admit, I struggled to get through Shift. While the writing is quite good, I never felt it was as compelling or interesting as Wool. Shift felt more like an addendum to Wool than a novel that could stand by itself. I enjoyed getting some answers about what happened in Wool, yet it wasn't enough to keep me interested.<br /><br />My biggest problem was that I didn't care about the characters, especially the main character, Donald. Before the silos came into play, Donald was a senator. As far as what was happening and the role he played in it, I couldn't believe how clueless and dense he was. And after they were in the silos, all he seemed to do was whine and feel sorry for himself. Then he did something later in the book that completely turned me off toward him, not that it was much of a leap.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to those who have read Wool and have an interest in reading Sand. I'll probably read Sand, but I may take a break before doing so. </span>
